You are a coding agent solving a grid-based puzzle game.

Game overview:
- You are solving a multi-level grid puzzle game. 
- You are called repeatedly over the course of a run. Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:

Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.

# Inspect the current frame segmentation
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
adj = seg['adjacency_list']

# Summarize nodes by color
from collections import defaultdict
color_nodes = defaultdict(list)
for n in nodes:
    color_nodes[n['color']].append(n)

print("Colors and node counts:")
for color, nlist in sorted(color_nodes.items()):
    print(f"  {color}: {len(nlist)} nodes, total pixels: {sum(n['pixels'] for n in nlist)}")

# Find blue and green nodes
blue_nodes = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'b']
green_nodes = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'N']  # light green

print(f"\nBlue nodes: {len(blue_nodes)}")
for n in blue_nodes:
    print(f"  id={n['id']}, pixels={n['pixels']}, boundary={n['boundary']}")

print(f"\nGreen nodes: {len(green_nodes)}")
for n in green_nodes:
    print(f"  id={n['id']}, pixels={n['pixels']}, boundary={n['boundary']}")

# Also check for other colored nodes
for color in ['S', 'w', 'g', 'G', 'c', 'W']:
    cn = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == color]
    if cn:
        print(f"\n{color} nodes: {len(cn)}")
        for n in cn:
            print(f"  id={n['id']}, pixels={n['pixels']}")

# Let me understand the maze structure better
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']
adj = seg['adjacency_list']

# Get node positions (centroid from boundary)
def get_center(node):
    b = node['boundary']
    rows = [p[0] for p in b]
    cols = [p[1] for p in b]
    return (sum(rows)/len(rows), sum(cols)/len(cols))

# Build adjacency info
print("Adjacency pairs involving key nodes:")
blue_id = 1
green_id = 66

# Find all adjacencies for blue and green
for a, b in adj:
    if a == blue_id or b == blue_id:
        other = b if a == blue_id else a
        other_node = nodes[other]
        print(f"  Blue({blue_id}) <-> {other} (color={other_node['color']}, pixels={other_node['pixels']})")
    if a == green_id or b == green_id:
        other = b if a == green_id else a
        other_node = nodes[other]
        print(f"  Green({green_id}) <-> {other} (color={other_node['color']}, pixels={other_node['pixels']})")

# Print all adjacency list
print(f"\nTotal adjacency pairs: {len(adj)}")
print("First 20 adj pairs:", adj[:20])
</parameter>
</function>

# Find the bounds of the maze
maze_rows = []
for i in range(64):
    row = ascii_grid[i]
    if any(c != 'B' for c in row):
        maze_rows.append(i)

print(f"Non-black rows: {min(maze_rows)} to {max(maze_rows)}")

# Find non-black columns
maze_cols = set()
for i in range(64):
    for j, c in enumerate(ascii_grid[i]):
        if c != 'B':
            maze_cols.add(j)
print(f"Non-black cols: {min(maze_cols)} to {max(maze_cols)}")

# Create a cell grid (each cell is 3x3)
# The maze seems to start at row 15, col 15
# Let's divide into 3x3 cells
cell_start_row = 15
cell_start_col = 15
cell_size = 3

# Determine grid dimensions
max_row = max(maze_rows)
max_col = max(maze_cols)
grid_h = (max_row - cell_start_row + 1 + cell_size - 1) // cell_size
grid_w = (max_col - cell_start_col + 1 + cell_size - 1) // cell_size
print(f"Grid dimensions: {grid_h}x{grid_w}")

# Extract cell contents
cell_grid = []
for gr in range(grid_h):
    row_cells = []
    for gc in range(grid_w):
        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        # Sample the center of the cell
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        # Determine dominant non-B color
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells.append(''.join(sorted(non_b)))
        else:
            row_cells.append('.')
    cell_grid.append(row_cells)

print("\nCell grid:")
for i, row in enumerate(cell_grid):
    print(f"{i}: {row}")

<parameter=code>
# Build a proper maze grid and find a path from start to goal
# The maze is 11x11 (rows 0-10, cols 0-10)

# Re-extract the cell grid more carefully
ascii_grid = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

cell_start_row = 15
cell_start_col = 15
cell_size = 3

maze = []
for gr in range(11):
    row = []
    for gc in range(11):
        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            # Get the primary color (excluding B)
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            # Pick the most common non-B char
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

# Print the maze
print("Maze grid (11x11):")
for i, row in enumerate(maze):
    print(f"{i}: {' '.join(row)}")

# Find start and goal
start = None
goal = None
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if maze[r][c] in ('bc', 'b'):
            start = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal = (r, c)

print(f"\nStart: {start}")
print(f"Goal: {goal}")

# BFS to find path
from collections import deque

# Walkable cells: anything that's not '.'
def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < 11 and 0 <= c < 11 and maze[r][c] != '.'

# BFS
queue = deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
path = None
while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal:
        path = p
        break
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
        if is_walkable(nr, nc) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        dr = path[i][0] - path[i-1][0]
        dc = path[i][1] - path[i-1][1]
        if dr == -1: moves.append('UP')
        elif dr == 1: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif dc == -1: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif dc == 1: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")
</parameter>
</function>
</tool_call>

[SYSTEM PROMPT]
You are a coding agent solving a grid-based puzzle game.

Game overview:
- You are solving a multi-level grid puzzle game. 
- You are called repeatedly over the course of a run. Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

# Check current player position
seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

# Find blue node
blue_nodes = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'b']
green_nodes = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'N']

print(f"Blue nodes: {len(blue_nodes)}")
for n in blue_nodes:
    print(f"  id={n['id']}, boundary={n['boundary']}")

print(f"Green nodes: {len(green_nodes)}")
for n in green_nodes:
    print(f"  id={n['id']}, boundary={n['boundary']}")

# Re-extract the maze and find player cell
ascii_grid = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

cell_start_row = 15
cell_start_col = 15
cell_size = 3

# Find which cell the blue player is in
blue_center = None
for n in blue_nodes:
    b = n['boundary']
    rows = [p[0] for p in b]
    cols = [p[1] for p in b]
    cr, cc = sum(rows)/len(rows), sum(cols)/len(cols)
    blue_center = (cr, cc)
    print(f"\nBlue center: ({cr}, {cc})")
    # Convert to cell coordinates
    cell_r = int((cr - cell_start_row) / cell_size)
    cell_c = int((cc - cell_start_col) / cell_size)
    print(f"Blue cell: ({cell_r}, {cell_c})")

# Rebuild maze and find path from current position
maze = []
for gr in range(11):
    row = []
    for gc in range(11):
        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

print("\nCurrent maze:")
for i, row in enumerate(maze):
    print(f"{i}: {' '.join(row)}")

# Find start and goal in current maze
start = None
goal = None
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if maze[r][c] in ('bc', 'b'):
            start = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal = (r, c)

print(f"\nStart: {start}")
print(f"Goal: {goal}")

# BFS from current position
from collections import deque

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < 11 and 0 <= c < 11 and maze[r][c] != '.'

queue = deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
path = None
while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal:
        path = p
        break
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
        if is_walkable(nr, nc) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        dr = path[i][0] - path[i-1][0]
        dc = path[i][1] - path[i-1][1]
        if dr == -1: moves.append('UP')
        elif dr == 1: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif dc == -1: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif dc == 1: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")

You are a coding agent solving a grid-based puzzle game.

Game overview:
- You are solving a multi-level grid puzzle game. 
- You are called repeatedly over the course of a run. Treat each turn as one observe-plan-act cycle: re-understand the current state from the newest frame, update your working world model in Python, choose the next best action or short sequence against the goal as currently understood, execute it, and expect to re-evaluate on the next turn from the updated state.
- Your job is to solve the entire game by clearing every level, not just the current screen.
- Levels often build on earlier mechanics, but layouts and interactions can still change between levels.
- Optimize for as few in-game actions as possible while still being reliable.
- In this environment, boards are presented as 64 x 64 color grids rendered with ARC color symbols.
- Color legend: W=white, w=light gray, g=gray, G=dark gray, c=charcoal, B=black, M=magenta, P=pink, R=red, b=blue, S=sky blue, Y=yellow, O=orange, r=dark red, N=light green, p=purple.


Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

- Keep code snippets short and purpose-built rather than dumping large frameworks into one call.

- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.

# Re-extract the maze with correct understanding
ascii_grid = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

cell_start_row = 15
cell_start_col = 15
cell_size = 3

# Build the maze grid (11x11)
maze = []
for gr in range(11):
    row = []
    for gc in range(11):
        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

print("Maze grid (11x11):")
for i, row in enumerate(maze):
    print(f"{i}: {' '.join(row)}")

# Find start and goal
start = None
goal = None
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if maze[r][c] in ('bc', 'b'):
            start = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal = (r, c)

print(f"\nStart: {start}")
print(f"Goal: {goal}")

# BFS with sliding mechanics
from collections import deque

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < 11 and 0 <= c < 11 and maze[r][c] != '.'

def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    """Slide in direction (dr, dc) until hitting a wall"""
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while is_walkable(nr, nc):
        r, c = nr, nc
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    return (r, c)

# BFS
queue = deque([(start, [start])])
visited = {start}
path = None
while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal:
        path = p
        break
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if (nr, nc) != (r, c) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        pr, pc = path[i-1]
        cr, cc = path[i]
        if cr < pr: moves.append('UP')
        elif cr > pr: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif cc < pc: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif cc > pc: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
    print(f"Path cells: {path}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")

# Debug: explore all reachable positions from current player position
# First, re-extract maze more carefully

ascii_grid = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

cell_start_row = 15
cell_start_col = 15
cell_size = 3

# Build maze
maze = []
for gr in range(11):
    row = []
    for gc in range(11):
        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

# Find player and goal
player_pos = None
goal_pos = None
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if 'b' in maze[r][c]:  # blue player
            player_pos = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal_pos = (r, c)

print(f"Player: {player_pos}, Goal: {goal_pos}")

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < 11 and 0 <= c < 11 and maze[r][c] != '.'

def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while is_walkable(nr, nc):
        r, c = nr, nc
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    return (r, c)

# Explore all reachable positions
from collections import deque
queue = deque([(player_pos, [player_pos])])
visited = {player_pos}
all_reachable = {player_pos}

while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if (nr, nc) != (r, c) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            all_reachable.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

print(f"\nReachable positions ({len(all_reachable)}): {sorted(all_reachable)}")
print(f"Goal reachable: {goal_pos in all_reachable}")

# Print maze with reachable positions marked
print("\nMaze with reachable positions:")
for r in range(11):
    row_str = ""
    for c in range(11):
        if (r, c) == player_pos:
            row_str += "P "
        elif (r, c) == goal_pos:
            row_str += "G "
        elif (r, c) in all_reachable:
            row_str += maze[r][c] + "*"
        else:
            row_str += maze[r][c] + " "
    print(f"{r}: {row_str}")

- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.

# Re-extract maze and run BFS with 2-cell movement
ascii_grid = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

cell_start_row = 15
cell_start_col = 15
cell_size = 3

# Build maze
maze = []
for gr in range(11):
    row = []
    for gc in range(11):
        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

# Find player and goal
player_pos = None
goal_pos = None
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if 'b' in maze[r][c]:
            player_pos = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal_pos = (r, c)

print(f"Player: {player_pos}, Goal: {goal_pos}")

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < 11 and 0 <= c < 11 and maze[r][c] != '.'

# BFS with 2-cell movement
from collections import deque

def get_moves(r, c):
    """Get all valid positions after one move (2 cells in a direction)"""
    moves = []
    for dr, dc, move in [(-2, 0, 'UP'), (2, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -2, 'LEFT'), (0, 2, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
        if is_walkable(nr, nc):
            moves.append(((nr, nc), move))
    return moves

queue = deque([(player_pos, [player_pos])])
visited = {player_pos}
path = None

while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal_pos:
        path = p
        break
    for (nr, nc), move in get_moves(r, c):
        if (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        pr, pc = path[i-1]
        cr, cc = path[i]
        if cr < pr: moves.append('UP')
        elif cr > pr: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif cc < pc: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif cc > pc: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
    print(f"Path: {path}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found with 2-cell movement!")
    
    # Try 1-cell movement
    print("\nTrying 1-cell movement...")
    def get_moves_1(r, c):
        moves = []
        for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
            nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
            if is_walkable(nr, nc):
                moves.append(((nr, nc), move))
        return moves
    
    queue = deque([(player_pos, [player_pos])])
    visited = {player_pos}
    path = None
    
    while queue:
        (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
        if (r, c) == goal_pos:
            path = p
            break
        for (nr, nc), move in get_moves_1(r, c):
            if (nr, nc) not in visited:
                visited.add((nr, nc))
                queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))
    
    if path:
        print(f"Path found with 1-cell! Length: {len(path)-1}")
        moves = []
        for i in range(1, len(path)):
            pr, pc = path[i-1]
            cr, cc = path[i]
            if cr < pr: moves.append('UP')
            elif cr > pr: moves.append('DOWN')
            elif cc < pc: moves.append('LEFT')
            elif cc > pc: moves.append('RIGHT')
        print(f"Moves: {moves}")
    else:
        print("No path with 1-cell either!")

Interesting! Looking at the transitions:
- Transition 0: RIGHT -> cell (0, 2) ✓
- Transition 1: DOWN -> cell (2, 2) ✓
- Transition 2: DOWN -> cell (4, 2) ✓
- Transition 3: DOWN -> cell (4, 2) - NO MOVEMENT!
- Transition 4: DOWN -> cell (4, 2) - NO MOVEMENT!
- Transition 5: RIGHT -> cell (4, 4) ✓
- Transition 6: RIGHT -> cell (4, 4) - NO MOVEMENT!
- Transition 7: DOWN -> cell (4, 4) - NO MOVEMENT!
- Transition 8: RIGHT -> cell (4, 4) - NO MOVEMENT!
- Transition 9: RIGHT -> cell (4, 4) - NO MOVEMENT!
So the player moved:
- (2, 2) → (4, 2) → (4, 2) → (4, 2) → (4, 4) → (4, 4) → (4, 4) → (4, 4) → (4, 4)
This means:
- From (4, 2), DOWN didn't work (player stayed at (4, 2))
- From (4, 2), RIGHT worked (player moved to (4, 4))
- From (4, 4), RIGHT didn't work (player stayed at (4, 4))
- From (4, 4), DOWN didn't work (player stayed at (4, 4))
So the player can only move in certain directions from certain positions. This suggests the movement is not simply "2 cells in direction" but something more complex.
Looking at the maze:

# Re-extract maze and run BFS with correct mechanic
ascii_grid = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

cell_start_row = 15
cell_start_col = 15
cell_size = 3

# Build maze
maze = []
for gr in range(11):
    row = []
    for gc in range(11):
        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

# Find player and goal
player_pos = None
goal_pos = None
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if 'b' in maze[r][c]:
            player_pos = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal_pos = (r, c)

print(f"Player: {player_pos}, Goal: {goal_pos}")

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < 11 and 0 <= c < 11 and maze[r][c] != '.'

def is_white(r, c):
    return is_walkable(r, c) and maze[r][c] in ('W', 'N', 'b')  # white, goal, or player

def is_gray(r, c):
    return is_walkable(r, c) and maze[r][c] == 'g'

def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    """Slide in direction, skipping gray cells, stop at white cell or wall"""
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while is_walkable(nr, nc):
        if is_white(nr, nc):
            return (nr, nc)  # Found white cell, move here
        elif is_gray(nr, nc):
            nr, nc = nr + dr, nc + dc  # Skip gray, continue
        else:
            return (r, c)  # Unknown cell type, don't move
    return (r, c)  # Hit wall, don't move

# BFS
from collections import deque

queue = deque([(player_pos, [player_pos])])
visited = {player_pos}
path = None

while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal_pos:
        path = p
        break
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if (nr, nc) != (r, c) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        pr, pc = path[i-1]
        cr, cc = path[i]
        if cr < pr: moves.append('UP')
        elif cr > pr: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif cc < pc: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif cc > pc: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
    print(f"Path: {path}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")
    
    # Show reachable positions
    print(f"Reachable: {sorted(visited)}")

Runtime variables inside every `python` tool call:
- `current_frame` is a lightweight frame view for the latest environment state.
- `current_frame` exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.

Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!

        r0, r1 = cell_start_row + gr * cell_size, cell_start_row + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = cell_start_col + gc * cell_size, cell_start_col + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

# Find player and goal
player_pos = None
goal_pos = None
for r in range(11):
    for c in range(11):
        if 'b' in maze[r][c]:
            player_pos = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal_pos = (r, c)

print(f"Player: {player_pos}, Goal: {goal_pos}")
print("\nMaze:")
for r in range(11):
    print(f"Row {r}: {' '.join(maze[r])}")

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < 11 and 0 <= c < 11 and maze[r][c] != '.'

def is_white(r, c):
    return is_walkable(r, c) and maze[r][c] in ('W', 'N', 'b', 'R')  # white, goal, player, or red

def is_gray(r, c):
    return is_walkable(r, c) and maze[r][c] == 'g'

def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while is_walkable(nr, nc):
        if is_white(nr, nc):
            return (nr, nc)
        elif is_gray(nr, nc):
            nr, nc = nr + dr, nc + dc
        else:
            return (r, c)
    return (r, c)

# BFS
from collections import deque

queue = deque([(player_pos, [player_pos])])
visited = {player_pos}
path = None

while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal_pos:
        path = p
        break
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if (nr, nc) != (r, c) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        pr, pc = path[i-1]
        cr, cc = path[i]
        if cr < pr: moves.append('UP')
        elif cr > pr: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif cc < pc: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif cc > pc: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
    print(f"Path: {path}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")
    print(f"Reachable: {sorted(visited)}")
</parameter>

- `current_frame.ascii` is a single newline-delimited string containing the latest board rendered with the letter-coded ARC color symbols.
- `current_frame.segmentation` parses the board into objects. It returns `{'nodes': [...], 'adjacency_list': [...]}`.
- Each node in `segmentation['nodes']` is one 4-connected same-color object with: `id` (index, ordered top-most-left-most), `color` (ARC color character), `hash` (a signature of the object's color and shape that ignores its position -- equal hashes mean the same object regardless of where it is, so use it to track an object across frames or to spot multiple identical objects in one frame), `pixels` (cell count), `boundary` (clockwise outer-perimeter corner points as `[row, col]`), and `children` (ids of objects fully enclosed by this one).
- `segmentation['adjacency_list']` is a list of `[i, j]` node-id pairs whose objects share an edge.
- `current_frame.step` is the current environment step count.
- `current_frame.level` is the current level number.
- `current_frame.shape` is a `(rows, cols)` tuple.
- The raw numeric grid is intentionally not exposed. Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, shapes, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes. Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region; do not scan the whole board with it.
- `history` is a chronological list of action/frame snapshots.
- `history` is a Python list of objects, not a dict.
- Each history entry exposes only `.action` and `.frame`; entries are not subscriptable like `entry['action']`.
- Each `history[i].frame` is the frame after `history[i].action`; each frame exposes only `.ascii`, `.step`, `.level`, `.shape`, and `.segmentation`.
- Important history semantics: when `history` is non-empty, `history[-1].frame` is the same latest/post-action board as `current_frame`. It is not the previous board. To inspect the state before the latest action, use `previous_frame` or `history[-2].frame` when available.
- `previous_frame` is the frame before the most recent real environment action, or `None` if no previous frame is available.
- `last_action` is the most recent real environment action name/display, or `None` before any real action.
- `last_action_frame` is the post-action frame for `last_action`; it matches `current_frame` after a real action.
- `transitions` is a chronological list of actual action transitions, excluding the initial seeded frame. Each transition exposes `.action`, `.before_frame`, `.after_frame`, `.frame` (alias of `.after_frame`), and `.result`.
- `last_transition` is `transitions[-1]` or `None`. Its `.result` mirrors `last_action_result`; older transitions may have an empty `.result`. For before/after diffs, compare `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`; do not compare `current_frame` to `history[-1].frame`.
- `last_action_result` is the persisted result dict from the most recent `action(...)` call. It remains available across later Python inspection calls that do not call `action(...)`, and is `{}` before any action result exists. Read transition metadata from fields/keys such as `last_action_result['board_changed']`, `last_action_result['done']`, `last_action_result['level_completed']`, `last_action_result['game_over']`, `last_action_result['run_complete']`, `last_action_result['reward']`, and `last_action_result['valid_actions']`.
- `valid_actions` is the current list of valid action names.
- Call `action(actions)` to execute one or more real environment actions from Python.
- Pass `action(actions)` a list like `['LEFT']` or `[{'action': 'MOUSE', 'row': 4, 'col': 7}]`.
- One action usually returns one frame, but a single action can result in a short multi-frame animation.
- After `action(actions)` returns, `current_frame`, `previous_frame`, `history`, `transitions`, `valid_actions`, and `last_action_result` are refreshed.


Multimodal context:
- User turns include an attached image of the current ARC grid.
- The image and `current_frame.ascii` are two representations of the same current frame.
- You can use images and other tools to understand the game state and guide your strategy, each may be useful depending on the current uncertainty.


Visual-game guidance:
- Treat each board as a scene with objects, blockers, targets, adjacency, containment, motion, and symmetry.
- Game entities are usually be rendered as connected multi-tile shapes such as 2×2, 2×3, 3×3, or longer patterned structures. Sometime they might also be 1x1 tokens.- Some games are logic or layout puzzles with no explicit player avatar or controllable sprite on the board. Do not assume a player exists; the relevant state may be an object, region, cursor, selector, or whole-board configuration.
- Background colors are often white or gray/black-ish large regions, but not always. Verify background hypotheses by area, stability, and object boundaries rather than assuming them.
- In many games, a long horizontal or vertical line near an edge is a timer or remaining-steps bar. It often shrinks or changes each step. If you identify such a bar, do not get distracted by it or treat it as core gameplay state unless there is concrete evidence that it interacts with the puzzle mechanics.
A common failure mode is to mistake a segmented edge bar for clickable puzzle pieces. If a repeated strip of small blocks sits flush against the top, bottom, left, or right border and actions only change that strip while the interior board stays the same, classify it as HUD/timer state, not as an object to click through segment by segment. DON'T DO THIS!
- Use coordinates only to target actions or describe local evidence. Do not frame the objective as reaching a specific absolute row or column.
- Re-ground on the newest frame after any score increase or abrupt scene change; the returned board may already be the next level.
- `WIN` means the whole game is solved. Mid-run level completion is more likely to appear as a score increase while play continues.
- Strategies may transfer loosely across levels, but layouts and mechanics can change. Re-check the new board before repeating a plan.
- For `MOUSE`, pass `row` and `col` integer arguments. `row` is vertical position, `col` is horizontal position.


Python tool guidance:
- Use `current_frame.segmentation` as your primary view of the board -- objects, colors, containment, adjacency, and cross-frame object hashes.
- Use `current_frame.ascii` only to read a small, specific region of the board when `segmentation` is not enough; never use it to scan or summarize the whole board.
- Every `python` tool call starts fresh. Re-import modules or re-define any custom utility logic you need.
- The only importable standard-library modules are: bisect, collections, copy, fractions, functools, heapq, itertools, json, math, operator, random, re, statistics, string.
- The only tool is `python`; call it with one ephemeral `code` string.
- Always inspect `current_frame`, `history`, and `valid_actions` from Python instead of reasoning from the raw board by eye.
- For the most recent change, compare `previous_frame` to `current_frame`, or `last_transition.before_frame` to `last_transition.after_frame`. `history[-1].frame` is the current frame, so comparing it to `current_frame` only compares the board to itself.
- Maintain a compact working world model: what entities or regions exist, what actions seem to do, what the goal likely is, what remains uncertain, and what plan best fits the evidence so far.
- IMPORTANT: Especially when the game is about making an agent navigate to a target, it is usually safer to write an explicit search algorithm such as BFS. More generally, when the objective is understood but the best action order is unclear, pathfinding, flood fill, BFS, DFS, beam search, shortest-path search, limited action-sequence search, or custom heuristics are all valid.
- Optimize for the shortest reliable sequence that advances the current goal as described by your world model. If confidence is low, program a discriminating probe and revise the world model from the result.
- Once the important state variables and action effects are sufficiently understood, stop probing and search in the inferred state space.
- Inspect current and history frames from Python instead of describing frames freehand.
- Never print or echo full board frames. Return only compact derived summaries such as object lists, diffs, coordinates, counts, or tiny local crops.
- Keep tool-output context size minimal and decision-oriented so you can quickly compare before/after state. It's fine to write a lot of python code, just make the output short and interpretable
- A strong default loop is: summarize the board, infer the desired environment change, write a small scorer or search over candidate sequences, execute the best probe or plan with `action(...)`, then inspect again until you understand exactly what changed.
- For object tracking, match objects by color, overlap, bounding box proximity, area change, and edge contact rather than by exact coordinates alone.
- For frame diffs, summarize changed cells, color transitions, appearing/disappearing components, movement candidates, and small local row slices around the changed region.
- After every action, verify whether gameplay objects changed or whether only a timer, progress bar, or remaining-step bar moved. Do not treat HUD-only changes as evidence that the move worked.
- Use `print(...)` for compact summaries, or assign a final compact object to `result`.
- Call `action(...)` inside Python rather than returning action text in the chat.
- `action(...)` accepts an ordered list of one or more actions. Once your code has selected a reliable sequence, it is often useful to batch it.
- You can also call `action(...)` multiple times in one Python snippet, including inside loops. Each call updates the preloaded variables before execution continues.
- If an action result reports `game_over`, `run_complete`, `level_completed`, or `done`, stop acting immediately and re-ground on the next turn.

# Extract maze with correct offsets
ascii_grid = current_frame.ascii.split('\n')

row_start = 21
col_start = 12
cell_size = 3
rows = 5
cols = 13

# Build maze
maze = []
for gr in range(rows):
    row = []
    for gc in range(cols):
        r0, r1 = row_start + gr * cell_size, row_start + (gr + 1) * cell_size
        c0, c1 = col_start + gc * cell_size, col_start + (gc + 1) * cell_size
        chars = set()
        for r in range(r0, min(r1, 64)):
            for c in range(c0, min(c1, 64)):
                if c < len(ascii_grid[r]):
                    chars.add(ascii_grid[r][c])
        non_b = chars - {'B'}
        if non_b:
            row_cells = list(non_b)
            row.append(row_cells[0] if len(row_cells) == 1 else ''.join(sorted(row_cells)))
        else:
            row.append('.')
    maze.append(row)

# Find player and goal
player_pos = None
goal_pos = None
for r in range(rows):
    for c in range(cols):
        if 'b' in maze[r][c]:
            player_pos = (r, c)
        elif maze[r][c] == 'N':
            goal_pos = (r, c)

print(f"Player: {player_pos}, Goal: {goal_pos}")
print("\nMaze:")
for r in range(rows):
    print(f"Row {r}: {' '.join(maze[r])}")

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < rows and 0 <= c < cols and maze[r][c] != '.'

def is_white(r, c):
    return is_walkable(r, c) and maze[r][c] in ('W', 'N', 'b', 'R')

def is_gray(r, c):
    return is_walkable(r, c) and maze[r][c] == 'g'

def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while is_walkable(nr, nc):
        if is_white(nr, nc):
            return (nr, nc)
        elif is_gray(nr, nc):
            nr, nc = nr + dr, nc + dc
        else:
            return (r, c)
    return (r, c)

# BFS
from collections import deque

queue = deque([(player_pos, [player_pos])])
visited = {player_pos}
path = None

while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal_pos:
        path = p
        break
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if (nr, nc) != (r, c) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"\nPath found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        pr, pc = path[i-1]
        cr, cc = path[i]
        if cr < pr: moves.append('UP')
        elif cr > pr: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif cc < pc: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif cc > pc: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
    print(f"Path: {path}")
else:
    print("\nNo path found!")
    print(f"Reachable: {sorted(visited)}")

# Update is_white to include red and purple cells
maze = [
    ['.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','N'],
    ['.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','.','g'],
    ['W','g','W','g','W','g','W','g','Rp','g','W','g','W'],
    ['g','.','.','.','g','.','g','.','g','.','.','.','.'],
    ['bc','.','.','.','W','g','W','g','W','.','.','.','.']
]

rows = 5
cols = 13
player_pos = (4, 0)
goal_pos = (0, 12)

def is_walkable(r, c):
    return 0 <= r < rows and 0 <= c < cols and maze[r][c] != '.'

def is_white(r, c):
    # Include W, N, b, R, p, and combinations
    cell = maze[r][c]
    return is_walkable(r, c) and any(ch in cell for ch in 'WNbRp')

def is_gray(r, c):
    return is_walkable(r, c) and maze[r][c] == 'g'

def slide(r, c, dr, dc):
    nr, nc = r + dr, c + dc
    while is_walkable(nr, nc):
        if is_white(nr, nc):
            return (nr, nc)
        elif is_gray(nr, nc):
            nr, nc = nr + dr, nc + dc
        else:
            return (r, c)
    return (r, c)

# BFS
from collections import deque

queue = deque([(player_pos, [player_pos])])
visited = {player_pos}
path = None

while queue:
    (r, c), p = queue.popleft()
    if (r, c) == goal_pos:
        path = p
        break
    for dr, dc, move in [(-1, 0, 'UP'), (1, 0, 'DOWN'), (0, -1, 'LEFT'), (0, 1, 'RIGHT')]:
        nr, nc = slide(r, c, dr, dc)
        if (nr, nc) != (r, c) and (nr, nc) not in visited:
            visited.add((nr, nc))
            queue.append(((nr, nc), p + [(nr, nc)]))

if path:
    print(f"Path found! Length: {len(path)-1} steps")
    moves = []
    for i in range(1, len(path)):
        pr, pc = path[i-1]
        cr, cc = path[i]
        if cr < pr: moves.append('UP')
        elif cr > pr: moves.append('DOWN')
        elif cc < pc: moves.append('LEFT')
        elif cc > pc: moves.append('RIGHT')
    print(f"Moves: {moves}")
    print(f"Path: {path}")
else:
    print("No path found even with red cells included!")
    print(f"Reachable: {sorted(visited)}")

seg = current_frame.segmentation
nodes = seg['nodes']

# Find player
blue_nodes = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'b']
for n in blue_nodes:
    b = n['boundary']
    cr = sum(p[0] for p in b) / len(b)
    cc = sum(p[1] for p in b) / len(b)
    print(f"Player center: ({cr}, {cc})")

# Check for pink bar (timer?)
pink_nodes = [n for n in nodes if n['color'] == 'M' or n['color'] == 'P']
print(f"\nPink nodes: {len(pink_nodes)}")
for n in pink_nodes:
    b = n['boundary']
    cr = sum(p[0] for p in b) / len(b)
    cc = sum(p[1] for p in b) / len(b)
    print(f"  Center: ({cr}, {cc}), pixels: {n['pixels']}")
